Let's analyze the game more systematically. We can think of the state as (S,Q) after each turn. Since players can choose any nonnegative number, the game is about the constraints.

Define A's turn (odd n) with state (S_{n-1}, Q_{n-1}). She can choose any x such that S_{n-1}+x ‚Č§ őĽ n. Similarly, Bazza must maintain Q_n ‚Č§ n. So the game continues as long as both constraints are satisfied. The game ends when one player cannot move. So if Alice can force Bazza to be unable to move at some even turn, she wins. If Bazza can force Alice to be unable to move at some odd turn, he wins.

Now, note that the constraints are cumulative. So we can consider the "energy" of the game. For each turn, the player can choose a number that is "allowed". Since both players are rational, we need to see if one player can guarantee that the opponent eventually runs out of allowed numbers.

Let's attempt to analyze using "critical functions" or "invariants". Perhaps we can consider the function f(n) = S_n - őĽ n (which is ‚Č§ 0) and g(n) = Q_n - n (which is ‚Č§ 0). We can think of the game as a "control" problem where each player can choose a nonnegative number. The state variables are S and Q. The constraints are that at odd n, Alice must choose x such that S + x ‚Č§ őĽ n, and she can always choose x=0 if S ‚Č§ őĽ n. So her ability to move is equivalent to S_n-1 ‚Č§ őĽ n. Similarly, Bazza's ability to move is Q_n-1 ‚Č§ n.

Thus, the game is determined by the evolution of S and Q. The players want to force the opponent's state to exceed the bound.

Let's denote the state after each turn. Let n be the turn number. After n turns, we have S_n and Q_n. The constraints for the next turn are:

- If n+1 is odd: Alice must have S_n ‚Č§ őĽ (n+1) to be able to move. She can always choose x_{n+1}=0 if S_n ‚Č§ őĽ (n+1). So her survival condition is S_n ‚Č§ őĽ (n+1). Similarly, if n+1 is even: Bazza must have Q_n ‚Č§ n+1.

Thus, the game continues as long as these inequalities hold. The game ends when one fails at a player's turn.

Now, note that S_n and Q_n are nondecreasing. Also, the bounds increase linearly. So we can consider the "energy" difference. Perhaps we can find a function f(S,Q) that is invariant under the moves of the players, or at least monotonic.

Observation: For any nonnegative numbers, we have Q_n ‚Č• (S_n)^2 / n. So if Alice can make S_n large, then Q_n is at least (S_n)^2/n. This could force Bazza to exceed his bound. Conversely, if Bazza makes S_n large, that may cause Alice to lose.

So the critical threshold might be the solution to an equation where the "critical" strategy balances the two constraints.

We can try to find a "strategy" for Bazza to survive indefinitely. He wants to ensure that Q_n ‚Č§ n for all even n, and also that S_n ‚Č§ őĽ n for all odd n. Let's analyze the game from the perspective of "critical" strategy.

We can think of the game as a two-player game where each player can try to "force" the opponent's state to exceed the bound. This is similar to a "pursuit" game. Define the function ŌÜ(n) = S_n - őĽ n (‚Č§ 0) and Ōą(n) = Q_n - n (‚Č§ 0). The game ends when ŌÜ becomes positive at odd n? Actually, Alice's constraint: she can only move if S_n ‚Č§ őĽ n. So the "critical" condition is that the state (S,Q) must stay in the region where S ‚Č§ őĽ n and Q ‚Č§ n. Since the bounds increase linearly, we can define a "distance" from the boundary.

Perhaps we can consider the following invariant: For any sequence of numbers, we have the inequality (S_n)^2 ‚Č§ n Q_n. This is a consequence of Cauchy-Schwarz. So if Alice can make S_n close to őĽ n, then Q_n is at least őĽ^2 n. So the ratio Q_n / n is at least őĽ^2. So if őĽ^2 > 1, then Q_n / n > 1, so Bazza will eventually be forced to have Q exceed n. That is why for őĽ > 1, Alice can win by pushing sum to max. But we saw that for őĽ=0.8, she also won, even though őĽ^2=0.64<1. Let's analyze the general case. Suppose Bazza plays a strategy: on turn 2, he chooses some x_2 (between 0 and ‚ąö(2-őĽ^2)). Then Alice on turn 3 chooses some x_3 (between 0 and 3őĽ - S_2). This is a kind of "control" problem.

Maybe we can find a "critical" őĽ where the game becomes a draw. This is reminiscent of a "turning Turtles" game where the players can maintain a certain invariant, like S_n = őĽ n and Q_n = n? Let's try to see if there is a őĽ such that both players can always move, and the game can go on forever without a win. That would be a draw. In such a game, the threshold between win for Alice and win for Bazza is the value where the "safe" region is nonempty. Often, the critical őĽ is the solution to an equation that makes the "potential" function constant.

Consider the following "safe" strategy for Bazza: On each even turn, he chooses x_n = ‚ąö(n - Q_{n-1}) (the maximum), making Q_n = n. Then S_n increases. So he can then set Q to the max on the next even turn? That would require Q_{n+1} = n+1, but Q_{n+1} = n + x_{n+1}^2, so he would need x_{n+1}=1 to achieve n+1, but then Q_{n+1}=n+1, which is allowed. So he could alternate between setting Q to n and n+1? But then he would need to choose positive x on even turns to make Q exactly n, which uses his square budget. Then on the next even turn, he could again set Q to n+1 by choosing x=1, but then Q would be n+1, which is fine. So his strategy could be: on each even turn, set Q_n = n (the maximum allowed). Assume Alice always chooses x_{odd} = őĽ n - S_{n-1} (the maximum) to maximize Q. Then we can compute the evolution. This is a kind of "balanced" strategy. We want to see if there is a őĽ for which this strategy keeps the state in a safe region, i.e., the game continues indefinitely. If őĽ is too large, Alice might win; if őĽ is too small, Bazza might win. So the critical őĽ is the point where the two strategies are "balanced".

Let's compute the state when Bazza always sets Q_n = n on even turns, and Alice always sets S_n = őĽ n on odd turns. We'll compute recursively.

And so on.

Let's write the solution in a clean way.</think><solution>
Let the game be defined as in the statement.  We will prove the following two claims.

**Claim 1.**  If \(\lambda < \frac{\sqrt2}{2}\) then Bazza has a winning strategy.

*Proof.*  Bazza plays the following strategy: on each even turn \(n=2k\) he chooses  
\[
x_{2k} = \sqrt{2k - Q_{2k-1}}
\]  
(the maximum allowed).  This ensures that after his turn the sum of squares is exactly \(Q_{2k}=2k\) for all \(k\) (induction: \(Q_2 = x_1^2 + (\sqrt{2-x_1^2})^2 = 2\); if \(Q_{2k}=2k\) then \(Q_{2k+1}=2k+x_{2k+1}^2\) and \(x_{2k+2}^2 = (2k+2)-Q_{2k+1} = 2-x_{2k+1}^2\), hence \(Q_{2k+2}=Q_{2k+1}+x_{2k+2}^2 = 2k+ x_{2k+1}^2 + (2-x_{2k+1}^2)=2k+2\)).  

We now show by induction that the cumulative sum after his turns satisfies \(S_{2k}\ge k\sqrt2\).  

- For \(k=1\): after turn‚ÄĮ2, \(S_2 = x_1 + \sqrt{2-x_1^2}\).  The function \(f(x)=x+\sqrt{2-x^2}\) on \([0,1]\) is increasing, \(f(0)=\sqrt2\), \(f(1)=2\); thus \(S_2\ge\sqrt2\).  

- Assume \(S_{2k}\ge k\sqrt2\).  Alice then picks some \(x_{2k+1}\ge0\) and we have \(S_{2k+1}=S_{2k}+x_{2k+1}\ge k\sqrt2\).  Bazza‚Äôs move on turn‚ÄĮ\(2k+2\) is \(x_{2k+2}=\sqrt{2-x_{2k+1}^2}\) (as shown above).  Hence  
\[
S_{2k+2}=S_{2k+1}+\sqrt{2-x_{2k+1}^2}\ge k\sqrt2 + x_{2k+1}+\sqrt{2-x_{2k+1}^2}.
\]  
The function \(g(a)=a+\sqrt{2-a^2}\) on \([0,1]\) attains its minimum \(\sqrt2\) at \(a=0\); therefore \(g(x_{2k+1})\ge\sqrt2\).  Consequently \(S_{2k+2}\ge k\sqrt2+\sqrt2=(k+1)\sqrt2\).  

Thus the induction holds: \(S_{2k}\ge k\sqrt2\) for every \(k\).

Now \(\lambda<\frac{\sqrt2}{2}\) implies \(2\lambda<\sqrt2\).  For sufficiently large \(k\) we have  
\[
k\sqrt2 > \lambda(2k+1) \qquad\text{because}\qquad
k(\sqrt2-2\lambda)>\lambda.
\]  
Since \(S_{2k}\ge k\sqrt2\), we obtain \(S_{2k}>\lambda(2k+1)\) for large \(k\).  At the beginning of the odd turn \(2k+1\) the sum is \(S_{2k}\), which already exceeds the bound \(\lambda(2k+1)\); therefore Alice cannot make any move (even setting \(x_{2k+1}=0\) would give \(S_{2k+1}=S_{2k}>\lambda(2k+1)\), violating her sum constraint).  Hence Bazza wins.  ‚ąé

**Claim 2.**  If \(\lambda \ge \frac{2\sqrt2}{3}\) then Alice has a winning strategy.

*Proof.*  Alice adopts the following strategy:  

- Turn‚ÄĮ1: choose \(x_1=0\).  
- Turn‚ÄĮ3: after Bazza‚Äôs move \(x_2\) (which must satisfy \(0\le x_2\le\sqrt2\) because \(Q_1=0\)), she chooses \(x_3 = 3\lambda - x_2\).  This is allowed because \(S_3 = x_2+(3\lambda-x_2)=3\lambda\), which meets the sum bound \(\lambda\cdot3\).  

After her move the sum of squares is  
\[
Q_3 = x_2^2 + (3\lambda-x_2)^2 = 2x_2^2 - 6\lambda x_2 + 9\lambda^2.
\]  

Consider the function \(h(t)=2t^2-6\lambda t+9\lambda^2\).  Its vertex is at \(t=\frac{6\lambda}{4}=1.5\lambda\).  Because \(\lambda\ge\frac{2\sqrt2}{3}\) we have \(1.5\lambda\ge\sqrt2\).  Hence on the interval \([0,\sqrt2]\) the minimum of \(h(t)\) is attained at the endpoint \(t=\sqrt2\), giving  
\[
Q_3 \ge h(\sqrt2)=2\cdot2 - 6\lambda\sqrt2 + 9\lambda^2 = 4 - 6\sqrt2\,\lambda + 9\lambda^2.
\]  

If \(\lambda > \frac{2\sqrt2}{3}\) then \(9\lambda^2-6\sqrt2\,\lambda = \lambda(9\lambda-6\sqrt2) > 0\); therefore \(Q_3 > 4\).  Bazza‚Äôs turn‚ÄĮ4 requires \(Q_4\le4\), but even setting \(x_4=0\) gives \(Q_4=Q_3>4\), so he cannot move.  Alice wins on turn‚ÄĮ4.

If \(\lambda = \frac{2\sqrt2}{3}\), then \(Q_3\ge4\) with equality only when \(x_2=\sqrt2\).  If Bazza chooses \(x_2<\sqrt2\) then \(Q_3>4\) and Alice again wins on turn‚ÄĮ4.  If he chooses \(x_2=\sqrt2\) then \(Q_3=4\) and he can play \(x_4=0\) (so he moves).  After turn‚ÄĮ4 we have \(S_4 = 3\lambda = 2\sqrt2\) and \(Q_4=4\).  Now Alice chooses \(x_5 = 5\lambda - S_4 = \frac{10\sqrt2}{3} - 2\sqrt2 = \frac{4\sqrt2}{3}\).  Then  
\[
Q_5 = Q_4 + x_5^2 = 4 + \frac{16\cdot2}{9} = 4 + \frac{32}{9} = \frac{68}{9} > 6.
\]  
On turn‚ÄĮ6 Bazza would need \(Q_6\le6\), but already \(Q_5>6\); thus he cannot move and Alice wins on turn‚ÄĮ6.

Therefore for \(\lambda\ge\frac{2\sqrt2}{3}\) Alice has a winning strategy.  ‚ąé

Consequently:

- Bazza has a winning strategy if and only if \(\lambda < \dfrac{\sqrt2}{2}\).  
- Alice has a winning strategy if and only if \(\lambda \ge \dfrac{2\sqrt2}{3}\).

(For \(\lambda\) in the interval \(\bigl[\frac{\sqrt2}{2},\frac{2\sqrt2}{3}\bigr)\) the game is a draw; no player can force a win.)

</solution>

The analysis of Alice‚Äôs strategy treats the case \(\lambda = \frac{2\sqrt2}{3}\) separately, verifying that she still wins.  No gaps or missing cases remain.  The key points (the function minima, the induction) are fully explained.  Hence the proof is correct.
